  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<span id="freeTextContainer1469476623098215937"><b>This National Book Award Finalist is a thought-provoking exploration of emotional abuse, self-reliance and the nature of evil. A heart-wrenching portrait of family crisis, this is perfect for fans of Laurie Halse Anderson’s <i>Speak </i>and Jay Asher’s <i>Thirteen Reasons Why</i>.</b><br/>  <br/> For Matt and his sisters, life with their cruel, physically abusive mother is a day-to-day struggle for s</span>
  <span id="freeText1469476623098215937" style="display:none"><b>This National Book Award Finalist is a thought-provoking exploration of emotional abuse, self-reliance and the nature of evil. A heart-wrenching portrait of family crisis, this is perfect for fans of Laurie Halse Anderson’s <i>Speak </i>and Jay Asher’s <i>Thirteen Reasons Why</i>.</b><br />  <br /> For Matt and his sisters, life with their cruel, physically abusive mother is a day-to-day struggle for survival. But then Matt witnesses a man named Murdoch coming to a child’s rescue in a convenience store; and for the first time, he feels a glimmer of hope. Then, amazingly, Murdoch begins dating Matt’s mother. Life is suddenly almost good. But the relief lasts only a short time. When Murdoch inevitable breaks up with their mother, Matt knows that he’ll need to take some action. Can he call upon Murdoch to be his hero? Or will Matt have to take measures into his own hands?<br /><br />National Book Award Finalist<br /> LA Times Book Prize Finalist<br /> ALA Best Books of the Year<br /> ALA Quick Pick<br />  </span>
  <a data-text-id="1469476623098215937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780739349083" href="/book/show/527069.The_Rules_of_Survival">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Catie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2443993-heidi-yup-still-here">Heidi (Yup. Still here.) </a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161078754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10855673082499386461">This ended up being a pretty personal review for me, so if you don’t want to read through my melodramatic reminiscing…I suggest you (ie, the three of you that read my reviews) skip this one!<br/>__________________________________________________________________<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/>It will be absolutely impossible for me to rate this book objectively. Did I like this book? Not just no, but hell no. However, I recognize that this dislike is influenced heavily by my own personal experience. I over-identified with the beginn</span>
  <span id="freeText10855673082499386461" style="display:none">This ended up being a pretty personal review for me, so if you don’t want to read through my melodramatic reminiscing…I suggest you (ie, the three of you that read my reviews) skip this one!<br />__________________________________________________________________<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />It will be absolutely impossible for me to rate this book objectively. Did I like this book? Not just no, but hell no. However, I recognize that this dislike is influenced heavily by my own personal experience. I over-identified with the beginning of the book, to the extent of making myself nauseous, but the middle and ending felt so unreal that I couldn’t engage with the story anymore. I do think that this book is well written, and I know that if I didn’t have the childhood past that I do, I would have probably liked it. So, I am settling on three stars.<br /><br />This story is written as a letter from an eighteen year old Matthew to his now nine year old sister, Emmy, recounting events from her early childhood, which she does not fully remember. Matthew and his sister Callie have lived in constant fear of their mother for years, as she has become more and more violent and manic. They both have taken on protector roles for Emmy, shielding her from as much as possible, and bringing the brunt onto themselves. However, Matthew is losing hope. When they witness a man defend a young child from being hit by his father in a convenience store, Matthew begins to imagine that he can track down this man and bring him into their lives to rescue them. But, when Matthew’s mother finds him first, nothing goes as planned.<br /><br />Matthew includes information about their future here and there in the letter, so the reader will know right from the beginning that these kids eventually get away from their mother. I like this – not only because it relieves some of the intensity of the beginning, but because it focuses the reader on the growth of the characters and not so much on wondering about the eventual outcome. <br /><br />There are parts of the beginning of this book that are very familiar to me, and I can identify with all three of these kids. Like Emmy, I don’t remember very much of my early childhood. My memory is made up of violent snatches of scenes, where my father hurts my mother and ridicules my little brother. I learned to be invisible quite early, to escape his notice. He was a heavy drinker, and he left when I was six years old. He did not pay child support, even though the court had ordered him to do so. Like Matthew, I once confronted my father, pleading for help. My mother sank into a deep depression in my teenage years, with frequent bouts of intense anxiety. She feared all Western medicine and so put her faith in natural healers, psychics, and herbs. One day she called home in a panic to tell me that a huge earthquake was coming. She had been informed by a psychic. I was to pack a bag, and she would buy us plane tickets to get out of town. I spent the whole afternoon alternately hunched under my desk, or standing in my doorway, trying to decide which location was the safest. When she got home, she went to bed, acting like nothing had happened. This was one of many incidents that occurred over years, but this one sticks out in my mind because it was the time that I realized that the majority of what my mom worried about wasn’t real. I wrote my father a letter, telling him that she was not okay, and that we needed help with money too. We didn’t have enough food, and I would wear the same clothes to school every day, because they were the only ones that fit. My mom was too lost to notice that any of this was happening. I’ll never forget his response. <i>I know you think that you’re “poor”….</i> Yes, he even used the quotation marks. Just writing this down, I still have a little spark of rage about it all. And, I can relate to Callie. Like Callie, I decided that keeping my head down and doing well in school was the one way to get me far away from everything. So yes, I can definitely “over-identify much?” with the beginning of this book. However, somewhere in the middle of this book, the characters’ reactions and emotional states start to seem unreal to me. For example, when Matthew’s father comes to him, finally willing to try and help, Matthew is simply relieved and happy. Where’s the anger? The betrayal of fourteen years spent without help? I can’t understand that. And, while I do find it inspiring that Matthew in particular could be so heroic for so many years, protecting and shielding his sisters, I find that hard to buy into as well (at least not without him suffering lasting damage from it, which he doesn’t appear to). I am not saying that it doesn’t happen, because I’m sure that it does. But, I barely had the wherewithal to get <i>myself</i> out of my childhood. I kept my head down, and my eyes focused inward, even as my little brother self-destructed right next to me. Yes, I still have some brand of survivor’s guilt about it, and this book does not help me.<br /><br />All three kids in this story survive over a decade of abuse and seem to have very little lasting damage. Yes, we only get to see up to Matthew’s eighteenth birthday, but the extent to which all three of these kids are thriving and whole is unbelievable to me. Now, let me just say (again), that my own personal experience is heavily coloring this opinion. Maybe there are kids out there that have been raised in violent environments and emerged healthy and whole enough to win scholarships have great relationships with other parental figures. I don’t know, but to me it seems unrealistic. I still struggle with my past and I am almost thirty years old. Only in the past few years have I been able to say things like, “she did the best that she could” and actually mean them. Only recently have I been able to see that my parents are just people, who had problems, which had absolutely nothing to do with me. My mom lived for years in an abusive relationship, and lost her father, her brother, and her first child within two years. I defy anyone to be mentally healthy after that.<br /><br />I initially liked the ending reveal, but the more I think about it, the more I hate it. To me it smacks of hiding away the dirty past and treating the mother as a simple villain. There’s one thing that I’ve definitely learned in my adulthood (which is one of the main reasons I don’t mind posting all of this information about my life on the internet): trying to hide or minimize the difficult past is a sure way to increase its influence. Whereas, if you share your past openly and stare it right in the face, it will lose its power. I would like to see Matthew and his sisters working on healing and struggling, without it happening instantly. I am in a very happy place in my life, but I still have healing to do. It’s not something that happens all at once, but in little moments throughout my life. Every time I take my daughters to the doctor for a regular check-up, or pick them up from school on time, or cook them dinner, or see my eldest become more and more of a daddy’s girl, it heals me.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10855673082499386461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I loved the taut suspense of this story--a story that will resonate with many teens who must deal with abusive parents. Matthew is faced with protecting his sisters from his unpredictable and often violent mother (who likely is afflicted with manic depressive disorder). Often he must bear the burden of her rage himself in order to to deflect it from the most vulnerable member of his family--his youngest sister, Emmy. The book is written from Matth</span>
  <span id="freeText12428662624194460802" style="display:none">I devoured this book in practically one sitting. I loved the taut suspense of this story--a story that will resonate with many teens who must deal with abusive parents. Matthew is faced with protecting his sisters from his unpredictable and often violent mother (who likely is afflicted with manic depressive disorder). Often he must bear the burden of her rage himself in order to to deflect it from the most vulnerable member of his family--his youngest sister, Emmy. The book is written from Matthew's point of view, as a letter to Emmy, who was too young to understand much of what was happening when they were living with their mother. Although the children are eventually rescued, there is still much healing to be done and writing the story is Matthew's way of purging much of his own pain.</span>

  <span id="freeText16513022030776066230" style="display:none">"The Rules of Survival" is about Matthew Walsh who lives with his two younger sisters and their abusive mother. He retells his story through a letter that he intends to give to his youngest sister Emmy, who was too young to understand the events that went on when Nikki abused them and put them through more than just their fair share of pain.<br /><br />This was such a good novel, I read it in only two days (both school days), so you can tell how addicted I was to reading it. I have never heard of Nancy Werlin before, but I'm definetly going to look at her other novels because this one came out of nowhere and impressed me. A lot.</span>

<div id="review_16601208" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Dave" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47354-dave"><img alt="Dave" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208179191p2/47354.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16601208?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave" href="/user/show/47354-dave">Dave</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">family or friends of mental patients, teens.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16601208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9002277226079124364">My youngest daughter picked this book up on CD at our library. It's a first person narrative in the form of a letter from an older brother to his youngest sister recounting a troubling and turbulent period when the two of them, and another sister, between them in age, were living with their mentally ill mother. The story held my attention with its taut dark psychological tension. Since I have close relatives and friends with significant mental health problems, I found the events and behavior des</span>
  <span id="freeText9002277226079124364" style="display:none">My youngest daughter picked this book up on CD at our library. It's a first person narrative in the form of a letter from an older brother to his youngest sister recounting a troubling and turbulent period when the two of them, and another sister, between them in age, were living with their mentally ill mother. The story held my attention with its taut dark psychological tension. Since I have close relatives and friends with significant mental health problems, I found the events and behavior described were entirely plausible. The form of the narrative as a personal letter drew me in as well. I think the book is a good one for teens/young adults to have a window into family dysfunction and the impact of untreated mental illness on the sufferer and their family. It is not a pleasant read, nor is it for the faint of heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9002277226079124364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1554574">
  <td colspan="2">

<span id="freeTextContainer14536685781037246283">This book is really something. Thought provoking, powerful, haunting. <br/><br/>Because the story took place over the course of five or six years, we see the steady growth of problems Matt, Callie, and Emmy endure and how they handle it. <br/><br/>What I loved most about this book was the realistic character development, particularly Matt's.   <span id="freeText14536685781037246283" style="display:none">This book is really something. Thought provoking, powerful, haunting. <br /><br />Because the story took place over the course of five or six years, we see the steady growth of problems Matt, Callie, and Emmy endure and how they handle it. <br /><br />What I loved most about this book was the realistic character development, particularly Matt's. Although he's a protective and dedicated to his sisters, he's still a kid. He shouts and gets upset and wants to leave and be selfish because he's just a kid. A kid put in the position of a parent and caretaker for his little sisters. And for the author to acknowledge this and incorporate it into the book makes it so much more real. <br /><br />Definitely would recommend and I wish there would be more books like this, that accurately portray the lives and feelings of someone in this situation. Very powerful. <br /><br /><i> “Always remember: In the end, the surviver gets to tell the story.” </i> </span>

<div id="review_185103983" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185103983" />
    <a title="Nina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5260179-nina"><img alt="Nina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369151886p2/5260179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185103983?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nina" href="/user/show/5260179-nina">Nina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781417818013" href="/book/show/6219358-the-rules-of-survival">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185103983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6635511189206577826">"Always remember: In the end, the survivor gets to tell the story."<br/><br/>This is the story of Matt, Callie, and Emmy, written as a letter. Emmy was too young to fully understand her past and so Matt has taken it as his responsibility to tell her about it. It's not only about an abusive mother but it involves outsiders who were dragged into it.<br/><br/>What did I think of the characters?<br/>WOW. This is one of the best character developments I have read. It didn't just occur suddenly but over a realistic period of </span>
  <span id="freeText6635511189206577826" style="display:none">"Always remember: In the end, the survivor gets to tell the story."<br /><br />This is the story of Matt, Callie, and Emmy, written as a letter. Emmy was too young to fully understand her past and so Matt has taken it as his responsibility to tell her about it. It's not only about an abusive mother but it involves outsiders who were dragged into it.<br /><br />What did I think of the characters?<br />WOW. This is one of the best character developments I have read. It didn't just occur suddenly but over a realistic period of time. I LOVE Matt. I view him as a hero. With the way he protected his little sisters... It may be something he just <i>had</i> to do but I felt as if it were something more than that. They all had their flaws and they all had times of vulnerability. Even Nikki had a side that was understandable. <br /><br />The writing, the story, just everything was so beautiful. I felt emotions the characters were going through. There were difficult topics in here. I loved how it talked about reasons why Matt didn't tell anyone about the abuse. Also, how someone can get used to an abnormal life somehow. The fact that it was written as a letter also made it different. The voice of the character was unbelievable. I actually felt as if I were listening to a teenage boy tell me his story. Definitely one of my favorites. <b>Undoubtedly five stars!</b> I look forward to reading more by Nancy Werlin and of this genre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6635511189206577826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30897244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18381688987580063969">A good account of a domestic life under siege. Matt and his sister Callie try to protect each other and their youngest sister from their unstable, irresponsible and clearly mentally ill mother, who can veer from manic cheer to vicious cruelty in seconds. The writing is pretty simple, but the short sentences and short chapters fit the edgy, survivalist mode of the story. I like that there is redemption (at a price), but not the kind of heroics the reader might expect. <br/><br/>I found it ironic (intention</span>
  <span id="freeText18381688987580063969" style="display:none">A good account of a domestic life under siege. Matt and his sister Callie try to protect each other and their youngest sister from their unstable, irresponsible and clearly mentally ill mother, who can veer from manic cheer to vicious cruelty in seconds. The writing is pretty simple, but the short sentences and short chapters fit the edgy, survivalist mode of the story. I like that there is redemption (at a price), but not the kind of heroics the reader might expect. <br /><br />I found it ironic (intentional?) that Matt's role model is named Murdoch ... the character reminded me of Matt Murdoch (Daredevil) from the classic Frank Miller era. </span>

<div id="review_1765601791" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1765601791" />
    <a title="Meg Kimball" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58859111-meg-kimball"><img alt="Meg Kimball" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522348186p2/58859111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1765601791?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meg Kimball" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meg Kimball" href="/user/show/58859111-meg-kimball">Meg Kimball</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1765601791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16096903449899477467">I didn't really hate this book, but I didn't understand the purpose of it. It did not entertain me. Instead, it kind of bored me. Not because of the subject matter (unpredictable, diabolical abuse, which is admittedly not boring), but because of the characters. I didn't care about any of them. The book didn't make me care. Of course I would care in real life, in the sense that I'd want to help them, but as a story, it fell really flat. Flat like a pancake. Read <i>Flowers in the Attic</i> instead! </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_123228225" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/123228225" />
    <a title="Mabel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4332766-mabel"><img alt="Mabel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285344082p2/4332766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/123228225?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mabel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mabel" href="/user/show/4332766-mabel">Mabel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer123228225" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14716960645148942177"> The Rules of Survival:<br/> Surviving the Unsurvivable<br/>	<br/> What would you do if you had an abusive and irresponsible mother? Would you tell someone? Pretend it wasn’t even happening? Make it stop? In The Rules of Survival, by Nancy Werlin, Mathew is the oldest of his three siblings who all have faced this problem every day of their lives. This realistic fiction novel is based in Boston, where the children have no one to turn to except each other, with an absent father, and an aunt who doesn’t seem to k</span>
  <span id="freeText14716960645148942177" style="display:none"> The Rules of Survival:<br /> Surviving the Unsurvivable<br />	<br /> What would you do if you had an abusive and irresponsible mother? Would you tell someone? Pretend it wasn’t even happening? Make it stop? In The Rules of Survival, by Nancy Werlin, Mathew is the oldest of his three siblings who all have faced this problem every day of their lives. This realistic fiction novel is based in Boston, where the children have no one to turn to except each other, with an absent father, and an aunt who doesn’t seem to know or care. Then a super-hero like man, Murdoch, comes into the picture, and makes all the troubles they have with their mom disappear. Soon enough though, he is thrust out of their lives as quickly as he was thrown in. Mathew is left with nothing but an even worse situation then before. With little hope left, he decides to take some action and make a difference in his and his sister’s life. I honestly rated this book with a four out of five, because it starts off a little slow, but once you get into it, you start to really feel right along with the all of the character’s experiences and emotions. The main character, Mathew, reminds me of myself because he doesn’t always show what he is really feeling, he has a natural motherly instinct towards the people he cares about, and he takes control when he needs to.<br />	<br /> Mathew reminds me of myself because often, he does not show his feelings, even to those closest to him. When he feels he needs to protect his sisters because of whatever their mother is doing, like driving the wrong way in traffic, doing drugs in the next room, bringing strange men over, or hitting him with various objects, he doesn’t show his feelings. He takes it, ignores it, and moves on, almost like he is trying to forget it even happened already, and so his sisters wont get scared or concerned about what is happening. I also cover up my feelings, sometimes just so I wont get extremely emotional in front of other people, and sometimes just because I want to deny that I'm even having those feelings.<br />	<br /> Another reason Mathew reminds me of myself is through his natural protective instinct towards his sisters and other people. I often find my friends telling me to stop acting like a mom. Mathew feels he has to do this for his sisters because of their lack of parenting and just to keep them safe from their mother's frequent rampages. Sometimes he hides his sisters and takes their punishment so they wont get hurt. He will do anything to keep them feeling secure, no matter what, even if he gets hurt because of it. We both protect people because of the instinct we share.<br />	<br /> Finally, I am reminded of myself by Mathew is because of how he takes control of situations. When things get way out of hand with his mother, he stops standing at the sidelines and takes a stand against her, finding a solution to keep her away. He acts when he needs to. He also leads his aunt to get help them. I also like to be a leader in many situations, another natural instinct I have. I always feel the need to do more and often to tell people what to do.<br />	<br /> Mathew, reminds me of myself because he doesn’t always show what he is really feeling, he has a natural motherly instinct towards the people he cares about, and how he takes control when he needs to. I really enjoyed this book. It sucks you in and you feel yourself getting emotional right along with the characters, feeling exactly what they feel. The story is easy to connect to, comparing it to some point in your own life. It makes you want to keep reading and really know what happens. I also liked the way it was written, as a book from Mathew to his sister, Emmy, just so she would know what happened during their childhood. I realized that even though my life may not be perfect, nobody has a perfect life. Also, that many people have it a lot worse then me, and I really should be thankful for what I have and my current situation.<br />	<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14716960645148942177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_201439778" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201439778" />
    <a title="Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3399699-rose"><img alt="Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386022874p2/3399699.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201439778?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rose" href="/user/show/3399699-rose">Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=tbr-challenge">tbr-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=abusive-relationships">abusive-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=ya-winter-challenge-4-2011">ya-winter-challenge-4-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201439778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1591279044175242129">This book terrified me, but I would say such in the most constructive way possible. Why? Well, for one, Nancy Werlin places the reader right in the center of 14-year-old Matthew's experiences in protecting his siblings from a mother from heck and back. The reader sees the desperation in his penned letters to his youngest sister Emmy chronicling the abuses they endured...and the desperation of getting out. If you're not aware of how terrifying Matthew's mother, Nikki, can be - consider some of he</span>
  <span id="freeText1591279044175242129" style="display:none">This book terrified me, but I would say such in the most constructive way possible. Why? Well, for one, Nancy Werlin places the reader right in the center of 14-year-old Matthew's experiences in protecting his siblings from a mother from heck and back. The reader sees the desperation in his penned letters to his youngest sister Emmy chronicling the abuses they endured...and the desperation of getting out. If you're not aware of how terrifying Matthew's mother, Nikki, can be - consider some of her actions as a parent:<br /><br />Driving into the opposite lane of traffic with her kids in the car, telling them that they have to declare their love for her as she swerves to avoid car, nearly killing them all.<br /><br />Making the kids gorge themselves on pancakes and then taking them to a Six-Flags theme park for "fun" (where the insinuation is to make them sick and if they become sick then they're not "grateful" enough.)<br /><br />Holding a knife to her son's throat while laughing.<br /><br />I think that's enough on the examples, but there are more moments to be shown through the story. In sum, Nikki is brutal and psychologically manipulative to a tee.<br /><br />The story introduces Murdoch, a man whom catches Matthew's eye because of the way he stands up for a kid being abused in the middle of a grocery store. Matthew sees Murdoch as a father figure, even emulates him to a degree. Unfortunately - during the brief time that Murdoch dates Nikki, he realizes what a destructive force she is and pulls out, which leaves Matthew back to square one, in a sense. The disappointment mostly sets in because Matt thinks that Murdoch can "save" them. I don't think it's a complete loss, because Matthew gets the seeds in his mind to find a way to help himself and his sisters escape the torment they endure, and it does involve fighting back by calling upon the people they learn to trust and having the strength to act beyond their mother's grasp.<br /><br />The tension carries itself through the story - I felt my heart beating double time in places because the story's so resonant with its emotional turns. The feeling that one doesn't know when Nikki will snap, and if one of the kids will be caught in the crosshairs is much like walking on eggshells as one moves through the book. There's an alternate edition of "Rules of Survival," different from my edition, where the cover depicts a bowl full of glass shards, which could very well sum up how much this book can get under your skin.<br /><br />There's also a quote in the midst of the book that somehow stuck with me - among many of the novel's moments, that I'll share here as taken from Matthew's words directly:<br /><br /><i>"But how come there don't seem to be any rules about when you ought to help others survive? Rules telling you when that's worth some risk to yourself?"</i> <br /><br />This hit me hard, because I think in the event of any abuse, it's important to take a stand, to tell, to talk about it. And I think one of the major themes of this novel is about gaining the strength to fight back in the face of adversity. I found it a wonderfully written, engaging story that hooked me from beginning to end, and it ranks among one of my favorite stories from Werlin.<br /><br />Overall score: 4.5/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="1591279044175242129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_104359316" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/104359316" />
    <a title="Alisha Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1585205-alisha-marie"><img alt="Alisha Marie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555610435p2/1585205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/104359316?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alisha Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alisha Marie" href="/user/show/1585205-alisha-marie">Alisha Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142410714" href="/book/show/3249404-the-rules-of-survival">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1585205-alisha-marie?shelf=books-i-own-that-i-ve-read">books-i-own-that-i-ve-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1585205-alisha-marie?shelf=tearjerkers">tearjerkers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1585205-alisha-marie?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer104359316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8932591607455538631">I HATED the mom in The Rules of Survival. So much that my blood pressure would literally spike while reading of the cruelties she inflicted on her children. I loathed the adults who turned a blind eye to the abuse because they just didn't want to deal with it at the time. In my book, by turning a blind eye, you become just as bad (if not worse) than the abuser. But yeah, this book had me pissed off through a vast majority of it and horrified when I wasn't pissed.<br/><br/>The Rules of Survival is one of t</span>
  <span id="freeText8932591607455538631" style="display:none">I HATED the mom in The Rules of Survival. So much that my blood pressure would literally spike while reading of the cruelties she inflicted on her children. I loathed the adults who turned a blind eye to the abuse because they just didn't want to deal with it at the time. In my book, by turning a blind eye, you become just as bad (if not worse) than the abuser. But yeah, this book had me pissed off through a vast majority of it and horrified when I wasn't pissed.<br /><br />The Rules of Survival is one of those books that doesn't shy away from the abuse inflicted. It was terrifyingly real, intense, and very emotional. I was cringing every time Nikki (the cruel mom) would strike her kids either with her fists or with words. This book hits a little close to home with me because I know what it's like to live with a person whose moods shift so quickly that you have to watch out for them and are constantly walking on eggshells to make sure you don't do anything to set that person off. So, I was rooting for Matthew, for Callie, and for Emmy and couldn't wait from them to get away from that suffocating and damaging atmosphere.<br /><br />While this book is all types of depressing, it is also hopeful because you know right from the first page that the children do survive and try to leave the past behind them. The fact that you know what happens from the first page, doesn't make this book any less gripping. It was entirely captivating. So much that I read this book in a little bit less than three hours. I was glued to my chair and didn't even get up once. I also loved that the The Rules of Survival was written in an epistolary format with Matthew writing to his younger sister, Emmy, whom he (along with Callie) tried to shield from the abuse as she was the youngest. I just thought that him writing to her was incredibly sweet and it brought tears to my eyes more than once.<br /><br />I highly recommend The Rules of Survival. You will get upset reading this novel. It's just inevitable. But there is a sense of hope in there as well. I hope that all of the Matthews, the Callies, and the Emmys of the world get the help they need and I hope with all my heart that they too become survivors. Because Werlin is right. The survivors are the ones who get to tell the story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8932591607455538631" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_743051019" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/743051019" />
    <a title="Lucy Martinez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24076867-lucy-martinez"><img alt="Lucy Martinez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379716735p2/24076867.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/743051019?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucy Martinez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucy Martinez" href="/user/show/24076867-lucy-martinez">Lucy Martinez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer743051019" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3832222688340794873">How can a mother love her children, but treat them like she doesn't at the same time? Most children feel safe around their mother, but not in this case. This is realist fiction story about Matthew and his two sisters that have to deal with having a dangerous mother.<br/><br/>There are a lot characters in this book, but without Nikki there wouldn't be a story. Nikki, the main character is the mother of her three children. She shows her love for her children differently, she's definitely dangerous and a bit</span>
  <span id="freeText3832222688340794873" style="display:none">How can a mother love her children, but treat them like she doesn't at the same time? Most children feel safe around their mother, but not in this case. This is realist fiction story about Matthew and his two sisters that have to deal with having a dangerous mother.<br /><br />There are a lot characters in this book, but without Nikki there wouldn't be a story. Nikki, the main character is the mother of her three children. She shows her love for her children differently, she's definitely dangerous and a bit insane. Nikki meets a guy named Murdoch and they began dating and her kids really liked him too. Nikki wanted them to be together and have fun, but Murdoch was beginning to realize who Nikki really is. So Murdoch broke up with Nikki. Murdoch doesn't get away from her that easily, she begins to say lies and makes up stuff to get him in trouble. This story is person vs person because Murdoch and Nikki's children have to learn how to deal with Nikki. Matthew, which is the oldest of the three is 14, Callie is 1 year younger and Emmy (their Half sister) is 6.<br /><br />The book, The Rules of Survival, is a very intense book. I really enjoyed reading it, its one of those books that once you start reading, you don't want to stop. I wish that the author would have identified who Nikki was in the beginning of the story because sometimes the narrator (Matthew) would call her by her name (Nikki) and sometimes 'Mom' which confused me at first. But I really liked who the author chose the narrator to be. He had a big part in the story and explained very well how he and his sisters had to deal with his mom. This story reminds me of the book The Outsiders because both Matthew and Ponyboy are writing a story about their lives for everyone to understand what happened in their life and how it changed.<br /><br />Overall, I thought this story is an amazing book and I strongly recommend it. Anyone who likes intense books, but also sad would like this. I would rate this book a 10 because it was amazing and i loved the characters and how in every page, there was something going on. Will Matt, Emmy and Callie get the family they always wanted or have to keep dealing with Nikki?</span>
  <a data-text-id="3832222688340794873" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText9435957418204576182" style="display:none">Reviewed by Sally Kruger, aka "Readingjunky" for TeensReadToo.com<br /><br />"You looked at Callie and me from over our mother's shoulder... I hoped you wouldn't try to wriggle out of the embrace, because in fact our mother seemed to be in a good mood. She was humming. Cocaine? New man? There were a few possibilities, and I didn't care which one it was. Maybe we'd have an okay evening." <br /><br />Those are the words of Matthew Walsh as he tells a story of survival to his youngest sister Emmy. Matthew has decided to write down the events as they happened so his sister can read them some day. She was so young at the time, he worries that she might not understand the details of their early life in the hands of an abusive mother. <br /><br />Nancy Werlin writes a powerful novel, THE RULES OF SURVIVAL, about three children and their struggle to live normal lives surrounded by chaos and abuse. Their crazed mother Nikki, most likely bi-polar or manic-depressive, has created a topsy-turvy environment for her family. One day she's treating them to a gigantic pancake feast at IHOP, and the next she disappears without a trace for days. <br /><br />Matthew, Callie, and Emmy soldier on alone until help arrives in the form of a sympathetic boyfriend. He enlists the help of Matthew and Callie's father and their mother's sister to rescue the kids from the chaos. <br /><br />Werlin creates a realistic and terrifying picture of life through Matthew's eyes. Readers will remember the story well after the last page. This is a book worth reading for adults as well as teens. </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer11094925406238964713">I believe the author knows about child abuse, neglect and a sociopathic parent.<br/><br/>Classified as Young Adult genre, the National Book Award Finalist is yet another book categorized as young adult simply because the narrator is a young child.<br/><br/>It is not easy to read about adult violence inflicted on children who desperately need assistance. Twelve year old Matthew Walsh desperately tries to protect his young siblings.<br/><br/>Their mother's mood swings, violence and cruelty is soul numbing and heartbreaking. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText11094925406238964713" style="display:none">I believe the author knows about child abuse, neglect and a sociopathic parent.<br /><br />Classified as Young Adult genre, the National Book Award Finalist is yet another book categorized as young adult simply because the narrator is a young child.<br /><br />It is not easy to read about adult violence inflicted on children who desperately need assistance. Twelve year old Matthew Walsh desperately tries to protect his young siblings.<br /><br />Their mother's mood swings, violence and cruelty is soul numbing and heartbreaking. The children live in constant fear of physical and emotional harm.<br /><br />When his mother purposely veers the car into an oncoming lane of traffic, Matthew knows he must do something.<br /><br />Contacting his biological father, family members and authorities only leads to more hurt and frustration. Not wanting to become involved, they look the other way.<br /><br />Fate brings Murdoch into their lives. Then, when Murdoch dates their mother, they have a wonderful summer...until, Murdoch realizes the psychopathic behavior.<br /><br />As Murdoch ends the relationship, he too becomes a victim of stalking and harassment from this very manipulative, sociopathic woman.<br /><br />Murdoch is the only one who can help them.<br /><br />While this is a dark tale, it is exceedingly well written and spot on regarding helpless children who deserve a better life.<br /><br />This is a book that leads you through a dark tunnel to find hope and light at the end.<br /><br />Highly Recommended</span>
  <a data-text-id="11094925406238964713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781616843229" href="/book/show/8252027-the-rules-of-survival">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5309738-kaje-harper?shelf=m-f-not-romance">m-f-not-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5309738-kaje-harper?shelf=intense">intense</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer750825649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9682905400135754772">A well-written and intense examination of living with an unstable and emotionally abusive parent. The book is written as a long letter from oldest sibling Matthew to his younger sister Emmy, containing both his recollections and resulting advice hard-won from the time when they lived with their mother. <br/><br/>The mother in this book is one of the most emotionally disturbing parents I've come across, almost too much so, because it was hard to think of her as a functional person. To her children she was </span>
  <span id="freeText9682905400135754772" style="display:none">A well-written and intense examination of living with an unstable and emotionally abusive parent. The book is written as a long letter from oldest sibling Matthew to his younger sister Emmy, containing both his recollections and resulting advice hard-won from the time when they lived with their mother. <br /><br />The mother in this book is one of the most emotionally disturbing parents I've come across, almost too much so, because it was hard to think of her as a functional person. To her children she was more of a force, like a windstorm always blowing, at different speeds and in different directions, but always threatening to turn full force on them unpredictably and rip their world apart.<br /><br />This book is also about the other adults who come into the children's lives, as Matthew searches for champions to help them. Not all are related by blood, notably Murdoch, whom Matthew latches onto as a potential savior. Through Matthew's eyes, the reader sees these adults cope with the difficulty in figuring out where responsibility lies when you are on the outside of that domestic disaster, looking in. There is no good way to deal with someone as difficult as Matthew's mother. Throughout the book, Matthew's determination to look after his sisters and to keep all their lives afloat is admirable, and kept my attention.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9682905400135754772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96685750" />
    <a title="Arlene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1746727-arlene"><img alt="Arlene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347048609p2/1746727.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96685750?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arlene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arlene" href="/user/show/1746727-arlene">Arlene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1746727-arlene?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1746727-arlene?shelf=read-2010">read-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96685750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8935180632765946301">What did I learn from this book? Well, a very obvious lesson which is the rule of survival at its basic level is self –preservation. When you’re in danger, your human instinct is to protect yourself. This story, however, showed me that as humans when we truly love someone, we’ll neglect our most basic instinct and protect those we love even if it endangers our own life. <br/><br/>This book is about three children who are being raised by an abusive mother. It was never quite clear if she was manic depressi</span>
  <span id="freeText8935180632765946301" style="display:none">What did I learn from this book? Well, a very obvious lesson which is the rule of survival at its basic level is self –preservation. When you’re in danger, your human instinct is to protect yourself. This story, however, showed me that as humans when we truly love someone, we’ll neglect our most basic instinct and protect those we love even if it endangers our own life. <br /><br />This book is about three children who are being raised by an abusive mother. It was never quite clear if she was manic depressive, bipolar, an alcoholic or drug user. As a reader, I feel I wasn’t given enough information to determine that, but I don’t think that was the point of this book. The story revolves around Matthew, the oldest of the siblings and his struggle to keep his sisters Callie and Emmy safe from their unstable, viscous and unpredictable mother. <br /><br />Truly a powerful story that’s both heartrending and daunting. When you turn the final pages, as a reader you hope that the lives of these children are not completely broken and maybe as time passes they will find peace and recovery in the aftermath of their struggle.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8935180632765946301" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2012355018" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2012355018" />
    <a title="Eshadi Sharif" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22311979-eshadi-sharif"><img alt="Eshadi Sharif" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483393792p2/22311979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2012355018?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eshadi Sharif" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eshadi Sharif" href="/user/show/22311979-eshadi-sharif">Eshadi Sharif</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2012355018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9241370333508710803">*actual rating: 3.5/5*<br/><br/>This was a really eye opening read for me. We overlook matters such as child abuse because it's an issue that's been going on for a long time-so long that even if we're not suffering from it, we feel like we're well informed about it. The Rules of Survival bring to light the many faces of it and in an indirect way, shows us what we're doing wrong when we stand on the sidelines watching it happen yet, not helping. <br/><br/>The first interesting thing I saw were the two portrayals of </span>
  <span id="freeText9241370333508710803" style="display:none">*actual rating: 3.5/5*<br /><br />This was a really eye opening read for me. We overlook matters such as child abuse because it's an issue that's been going on for a long time-so long that even if we're not suffering from it, we feel like we're well informed about it. The Rules of Survival bring to light the many faces of it and in an indirect way, shows us what we're doing wrong when we stand on the sidelines watching it happen yet, not helping. <br /><br />The first interesting thing I saw were the two portrayals of obsession. <br />•your typical stalker kind <br />•the kind where you're low key obsessed with a person because you find something in them that you don't have in yourself but desperately want. In other words, the obsession with hope in a time when its best to be hopeless. <br />It was very interesting because it really drove it's message home when you sat and considered the fact that there is a kid out there who will latch onto any speck of light in eternal darkness. <br /><br />In addition to the previous point, hope had a huge key role in this story. I was really impressed with Callie because despite not having the luxury of safety in her own home, she had the guts to aspire to become someone bigger, someone in her shoes wouldn't usually have the chance of becoming. <br /><br />Moreover, although I'm not sure of the accuracy of the portrayal in reality, but you get to see 3 different faces of abuse: Callie, who was on the verge of giving up with her fingers being the only thing keeping her on the ledge. Emmy, the stubborn, vindictive one with an anger issue. Matthew, the parent figure of his own life and his siblings, getting by by protecting and holding onto hope. <br /><br />The most interesting aspect is probably the extent to which childhood events and how much fear affects you. There come times when simple tasks seem unattainable due to fear or more precisely, fear instilled in you since childhood. Some people only hold the power of fear and it's just a matter of walking out that door holding you back from a better life. But you don't walk out because you're afraid-of what, you don't know. The same goes for Matthew, even as a boy stronger and bigger than the mother, he still fears her. <br /><br />This book also made me really question the mother-child bond. The bond is usually described as something pure and beautiful and maybe even primitive. There's something beyond the bond that surpasses all logical explanation. When asked, mothers will remark that mothers would never want to harm their children as if it's a universal fact. But what happens when that otherworldly bond doesn't reach some people? Nothing could prevent the mother from wanting to harm her child just for the heck of it. The Rules of Survival shows you exactly what happens when the bond doesn't reach a mother. However, you still know that bond exists but is only one way because Matthew couldn't resist going to his mom's aid even when he didn't have to in the last couple pages. <br /><br />The only con I'd say is how abruptly the father was willing to help. It was sort of weird because, well, even if he did have someone explain the situation, it was still weird how out of nowhere he was willing to help. I think if the author went into a depth a bit about that, it'd be more explainable. <br /><br />In conclusion, I'd say this is a great book which helps you understand the lives of the less fortunate a little more. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9241370333508710803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1904510952" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1904510952" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59559697-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473687863p2/59559697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1904510952?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/59559697-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1904510952" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4845183993057767731">Harebrained. That is the best word that comes to mind to me when I read this book. It describes Nicki Walsh other than insane, nuts and psychotic. If you enjoy books about people who are totally out there and insane then this book is just right for you! <br/>	The rules of survival by: Nancy Werlin is definitely a page turner because I could not just put it down I always had to keep reading! This book has a lot of very exciting and suspenseful moments but also has sad ones too. When I would be reading</span>
  <span id="freeText4845183993057767731" style="display:none">Harebrained. That is the best word that comes to mind to me when I read this book. It describes Nicki Walsh other than insane, nuts and psychotic. If you enjoy books about people who are totally out there and insane then this book is just right for you! <br />	The rules of survival by: Nancy Werlin is definitely a page turner because I could not just put it down I always had to keep reading! This book has a lot of very exciting and suspenseful moments but also has sad ones too. When I would be reading this book it made me put myself in the character's shoes and think what it would be like if I was in a situation just like the Walsh's. So overall I really enjoyed this book!<br />	I feel like for the most part the plot was believable but at times I felt like it was just too crazy to be true. For example when Nicki was holding her daughter Emmy over a cliff that plummeted hundreds of feet into the ocean. Would any mother do this? Crazy or not? Most of the story I feel is believable and could happen to a family but there were still some things that were just so unbelievable.<br />My favorite character in the story is Nicki Walsh. Even though Nicki is crazy and does some really mean and nasty things to her Children, sister, friends and just about everybody who knows her! It is always interesting to see what Nicki has up her sleeve and what dirty tricks she is planning to pull off next. There are also times you just absolutely hate Nicki Walsh. For instance, whenever Nicki hurts innocent people and causes pain to people are times when you honestly hate her! <br />I would recommend this book to someone who wants to read a very interesting book that is like no other book on the shelf and are interested in very odd and somewhat violent stories. You should also read this book if you are into stories that go from being sad to funny to just plain weird! That is what I think of The rules of survival by: Nancy Werlin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4845183993057767731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2356285683" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2356285683" />
    <a title="Donna Sawyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/77581069-donna-sawyer"><img alt="Donna Sawyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523319053p2/77581069.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2356285683?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donna Sawyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donna Sawyer" href="/user/show/77581069-donna-sawyer">Donna Sawyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">teens and parents of teens</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Donna by: </span>
      work colleague
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77581069-donna-sawyer?shelf=reading-with-daughters">reading-with-daughters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2356285683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6437052702062260280">This book was recommended to me by a work colleague as a YA novel to read/discuss with my teenage daughter. I ended up devouring it within a day.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/110535.The_Rules_of_Survival' title='The Rules of Survival by Nancy Werlin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Rules of Survival</a> is a well-paced story about three siblings deprived of a normal childhood and life by their abusive mother. It's told from an approachable first-person perspective of the brother, recounting their shared childhood via a "letter" to his younger sister, Emmy. I enjoyed this twist, which left me truly feeling like I was, indeed, Emmy.</span>
  <span id="freeText6437052702062260280" style="display:none">This book was recommended to me by a work colleague as a YA novel to read/discuss with my teenage daughter. I ended up devouring it within a day.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/110535.The_Rules_of_Survival" title="The Rules of Survival by Nancy Werlin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rules of Survival</a> is a well-paced story about three siblings deprived of a normal childhood and life by their abusive mother. It's told from an approachable first-person perspective of the brother, recounting their shared childhood via a "letter" to his younger sister, Emmy. I enjoyed this twist, which left me truly feeling like I was, indeed, Emmy. <br /><br />The book has all of the tense undercurrents you'd expect from such subject matter, and it is a page turner. The beauty of this book however is how you get to know the characters by their <i>behavior</i> -- their coping mechanisms, their self-sacrifice, and their own (varying) limits for fear and pain. The youngest child is the subject of protection, and as the story escalates is the reason things ultimately must change. Three key adults help progress this escalation, and while these characters were well-imagined I did find myself wishing for more of their backstory (they could probably each carry a novel of their own).<br /><br />The author details the longing of the children for a normal life in such a way that you feel the pain acutely, without it ever being directly described. They go from knowing their lives are in danger one moment to convincing themselves things are okay the next; it is in a word, heartbreaking. What will someone do to feel safe enough to dream again? Or is "safe" even possible, after all that's happened?<br /><br />Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6437052702062260280" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2176876088" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2176876088" />
    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/104823-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527527813p2/104823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2176876088?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/104823-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio 9780739355770" href="/book/show/13133863-the-rules-of-survival">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=realistic">realistic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=age-ya">age-ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2176876088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9574258553984817646">Matt is writing a letter to his youngest sister Emmy, telling her about what they all went through being raised by their abusive mother Nikki. Nikki doesn't beat them (much) or starve them, but she does leave them alone for days at a time and is emotionally abusive. Matt is used to dealing with Nikki, until he meets Murdoch, who stands up to an abusive parent in a store while Matt watches. He later decides to track Murdoch down, but Nikki beats him to the punch and ends up dating Murdoch. Even a</span>
  <span id="freeText9574258553984817646" style="display:none">Matt is writing a letter to his youngest sister Emmy, telling her about what they all went through being raised by their abusive mother Nikki. Nikki doesn't beat them (much) or starve them, but she does leave them alone for days at a time and is emotionally abusive. Matt is used to dealing with Nikki, until he meets Murdoch, who stands up to an abusive parent in a store while Matt watches. He later decides to track Murdoch down, but Nikki beats him to the punch and ends up dating Murdoch. Even after they break up, Murdoch helps Matt try to extract himself, Callie, and Emmy from this situation.<br /><br />When I read that this was about an abusive mother, I expected someone "more abusive" - but this meant that I could see where Matt was coming from, not believing his mother was "abusive enough" to contact social services. I think the weakest part of the book was Matt trying to find Murdoch and Nikki stealing that information and somehow ending up dating him, but I did like that Matt eventually had an ally who knew exactly what Nikki was capable of and how she operated. Matt is pretty harsh about his Aunt Bobby, who lives in the apartment downstairs, saying he'll never trust her because she didn't step up sooner, when Aunt Bobby had both grown up with Nikki's abuse against her, and how would she think it was "abusive enough" when Matt himself didn't think so? But kids aren't exactly understanding of what adults' lives entail, so it still rang true. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9574258553984817646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
